The standard threshold for obesity is defined as greater than or equal to 30kg\/m\u003Csup\u003E2\u003C\/sup\u003E (weight in kilograms divided by height in meters squared).\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EDaily food logs, recall questionnaires, and surveys are often used to measure the amount and consumption of food. The only option feasible at the population level are survey questions as they require less time and person-power to record the data accurately. Small studies may also use technical methods to measure the exact amount of nutrients consumed.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EResearchers have used a variety of strategies to measure the food environment ranging from geographic information systems to observational assessments. Studies of access to healthy foods generally look at access in terms of distance, income, and transportation. Foods such as fruits and vegetables are commonly available at grocery stores, supermarkets and farmers markets but not in convenience or small grocery stores where a large percentage of Americans purchase their food. [1,2]\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EMeasures of physical activity are usually taken from surveys for which a person self-reports the amount of time they spend participating in various activities such as aerobics, walking, biking, swimming, or jogging. Other measures of physical activity include using pedometers, accelerometers, or target heart rate measurements. However, these are not feasible on the population level across counties.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EResearchers have used several methods to capture important aspects of the environment that affect physical fitness and activity levels. One approach involves sending teams of auditors to communities to measure built environment factors using specific criteria and tools. To date, this method shows strong promise for collecting reliable and objective data; however, many communities do not have detailed GIS information available and the staff and resources needed to collect and analyze such data are expensive.[3]\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Ch5\u003EReferences\u003C\/h5\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E[1] Wrigley N, Warm D, Margetts B, Whelan A. Studies have used self-reported measures such as numbers and types of sex partners, frequency of sex acts, and condom use practices to try to measure unsafe sexual activity. However, discrepancies have been shown to exist between self-reports of sexual behavior and objective sexual risk behavior outcomes such as sexually transmitted infections.[1-3] Self-reported measures can also be unreliable, difficult to gather, and subject to errors in measurement, especially reporting and recall bias. This can especially be true for sexual activity, as sex is a highly charged issue which carries a stigma and can be heavily dependent on social norms.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EAs a consequence, it is easier to use proxy measures to represent sexual behavior. When addressing sexual risk behavior, the CDC points to promoting behaviors that can reduce risk for sexually transmitted diseases and teen pregnancy.[4] Sexually transmitted infections can be a result of unsafe sexual activities including those used in self-reported measures such as condom practices and number of sexual partners. Teen births resulting from unintended pregnancies are an additional way to measure unsafe sex. Unintended pregnancy mainly results from the lack of, inconsistent, or incorrect use of effective contraceptive methods, and among women aged 15-19, more than 4 out of 5 pregnancies were unintended.[5]\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E[1] DiClemente RJ, Sales JM, Danner F, Crosby RA. Accessed February 27, 2013.\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},"relationships":{"paragraph_type":{"data":{"type":"paragraphs_type--paragraphs_type","id":"e734b1ee-ccc4-4aee-bf85-23e2b1ea8dee","meta":{"drupal_internal__target_id":"content_block"}},"links":{"related":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/0e201cdb-a7c7-4136-8641-aa0a3c54755e\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A640"},"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/0e201cdb-a7c7-4136-8641-aa0a3c54755e\/relationships\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A640"}}}}},{"type":"paragraph--content_block","id":"285f3899-ae62-4d32-870b-cb7a94621187","links":{"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/285f3899-ae62-4d32-870b-cb7a94621187?resourceVersion=id%3A641"}},"attributes":{"drupal_internal__id":641,"drupal_internal__revision_id":641,"langcode":"en","status":true,"created":"2019-12-27T18:22:13+00:00","parent_id":"133","parent_type":"node","parent_field_name":"field_page_content","behavior_settings":[],"default_langcode":true,"revision_translation_affected":true,"field_wysiwyg":{"value":"\u003Cp\u003EAccording to the CDC, access to care includes four characteristics:\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Col\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003ECoverage \u0026ndash; There are several mechanisms in which to characterize injury, including injury mortality (measured by injury death data) and injury morbidity (typically measured by hospitalization and Emergency Department (ED) use data). The leading causes of injury differ based on how injury is measured. For instance, the leading cause for injury death in the U.S. in 2012 was poisoning followed by motor vehicle traffic crashes, whereas for non-fatal injuries, falls were the leading cause of injury ED visits [1].\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EAdditional ways to classify injuries are by intent and by setting. Injury data can be characterized as unintentional, intentional (such as homicides and suicides), and undetermined intent. Another way to measure injury applicable to the safety of a community is by setting, including occupational safety, safety in the home, and safety on the streets. Almost half of non-fatal injuries occur in or around the home, and although the number of occupationally related fatalities is a small subset of overall injury fatalities, these injuries are important to consider as they occur in a more controlled environment with a potentially better opportunity for targeted interventions [1,2].\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EOne issue that arises with injury measurement is the paucity of state-level data on the external causes of non-fatal injuries. Classification of external causes of injury has not been consistently defined or reported. As of 2009, only 26 states mandated routine collection of external cause codes (ICD codes) in their hospital discharge data sets. Improvements to the reporting and standardization of the classification of external causes of injury have been seen in recent years [2,3].\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EStudies exploring the impact of neighborhood violence on health traditionally measure violence in one of three ways: perception of neighborhood safety, exposure to neighborhood violence, or actual measures of crime or disorder [4]. Johnson et al. notes that studies that explore general health or physical activity tend to focus on perceptions of neighborhood safety, studies that investigate mental health tend to measure exposure to violence, and multi-community studies use actual measures of crimes or disorder [4].\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EThere is no gold standard for assessing crime rates at the neighborhood level, but some researchers have successfully used the Federal Bureau of Investigation\u0026rsquo;s Uniform Crime Reports because they are used consistently across government reporting agencies and are considered a valid and reliable index of the types of crime residents view as serious events [5]. According to the FBI, violent crime is defined to include murder and nonnegligent manslaughter, rape, robbery, and aggravated assault. Nonviolent crimes, including crimes such as drug abuse or counterfeiting, are often not considered in assessing community safety as they do not usually cause threat or physical harm to other persons.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Ch5\u003EReferences\u003C\/h5\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E[1] Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.cdc.gov\/injury\/wisqars\/leadingcauses.html\u0022\u003ETen Leading Causes of Death and Injury\u003C\/a\u003E. Among younger ages, standardized test scores, the percentage enrolled in pre-kindergarten, and truancy rates can be used to understand the level and quality of education taking place in schools. Adequate Yearly Progress, for instance, measures academic performance of public schools in the United States according to standardized test results, as defined and measured by the federal No Child Left Behind Act and the U.S. Department of Education. Adequate Yearly Progress includes requirements for standards of readings and mathematics, as well as attendance and graduation rates. Other common proxies for quality of education include measures of resources, in the form of either spending per pupil or specific school inputs such as the number of teachers per pupil, access to computers, or the availability of after-school programs. Although resources may be necessary for a high\u2010quality school, they may not be sufficient as some schools are likely to use their resources more effectively than others [1].\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EIn the adult population, the average years of education or percent with a high school, 2-year, or 4-year college degree is often used to understand levels of educational attainment. High school graduation is a frequently used measure but has several nuances. States measure high school graduation rates differently based on how they include transfer or new students and GED earners, and how they define the number of years allowed to receive a degree. In recent years, increasing attention has been paid to educational credentials and attainment other than academic degrees that have labor market value. In 2009, the Federal Interagency Working Group on Expanded Measures of Enrollment and Attainment (GEMEnA) recommended a set of measures including educational certifi\u0026shy;cates, professional certifications, and licenses to address the issue of shortfalls in the measurement of educational attainment [2].\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E[1] Ladd HF, Loeb S. The challenges of measuring school quality: Implications for educational equity. Education, democracy and justice. University of Chicago Press, Chicago. 2012.\u003Cbr\u003E\r\n\t[2] Ewert S, Kominski R. Measuring Alternative Educational Credentials: 2012. United States Census Bureau Report. Household Economic Studies 2014;70-138.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n","format":"full_html","processed":"\u003Cp\u003EEducation can be measured multiple ways depending on the age group of interest. line-height: 20.006303787231445px;\u0022\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cp\u003EThe Bureau of Labor Statistics Local Area Unemployment Statistics (LAUS) calculates six measures of unemployment:\u003Co:p\u003E\u003C\/o:p\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\t\u003Cul\u003E\r\n\t\t\u003Cli\u003EU-1, persons unemployed 15 weeks or longer, as a percent of the civilian labor force;\u003Co:p\u003E\u003C\/o:p\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\t\u003Cli\u003EU-2, job losers and persons who completed temporary jobs, as a percent of the civilian labor force;\u003Co:p\u003E\u003C\/o:p\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\t\u003Cli\u003EU-3, total unemployed, as a percent of the civilian labor force (this is the definition used for the official unemployment rate);\u003Co:p\u003E\u003C\/o:p\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\t\u003Cli\u003EU-4, total unemployed plus discouraged workers*, as a percent of the civilian labor force plus discouraged workers;\u003Co:p\u003E\u003C\/o:p\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\t\u003Cli\u003EU-5, total unemployed, plus discouraged workers, plus all other marginally attached workers*, as a percent of the civilian labor force plus all marginally attached workers; and\u003Co:p\u003E\u003C\/o:p\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\t\u003Cli\u003EU-6, total unemployed, plus all marginally attached workers, plus total employed part-time for economic reasons, as a percent of the civilian labor force plus all marginally attached workers.\u003Co:p\u003E\u003C\/o:p\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003C\/ul\u003E\r\n\r\n\t\u003Cp\u003EMeasures U-3 and U-4 are most commonly used. \u0026nbsp;\u003Co:p\u003E\u003C\/o:p\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\t\u003Cp\u003EAnother potential measure of unemployment looks at the number of people receiving unemployment compensation. This measure underestimates the number of people who are unemployed because not every worker has unemployment insurance and those who do are eligible only for a limited period of time, regardless of whether new employment is found.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\t\u003Cp\u003E*definitions of discouraged workers and marginally attached workers can be found \u003Ca href=\u0022https:\/\/www.bls.gov\/cps\/lfcharacteristics.htm#discouraged\u0022\u003Ehere\u003C\/a\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\t\u003Cdiv\u003E\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/div\u003E\r\n\u003C\/div\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cdiv style=\u0022font-size: 13px; Types of support, including companionship, emotional, tangible, and informational, can be categorized as perceived or received. Perceived support is shown to correlate with mental and social health as well as with length and quality of life [1]. Perceived support can be described as the \u0026quot;safety net\u0026quot;\u0026nbsp;feeling that when something goes wrong individuals can get the support they need. Received support is defined as the reported receipt of services and can be divided into informational, financial, emotional, belonging, or tangible support with specific measures developed to quantify each of these.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EIndirect support measures include living arrangements, availability of confidants, divorce or suicide rates, substantiated rates of child abuse or neglect, percentage of families who eat dinner together, parents who read to their children, volunteer rates, voter registration, percentage who read the newspaper, or percent of the population belonging to clubs or organizations such as bowling leagues, faith based organizations, and civic groups [2-4].\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EThe U.S. Census and American Community Survey collect data on household structures. Using these data, researchers can estimate the percent of children living in single-parent households or adults living alone, which serve as proxy measures of support. Other specific research groups have created measures of social capital which include measures of disconnectedness, isolation, whether basic individual needs are met, indicators of neighborhood trust, and adherence to civic norms [5]. The General Social Survey (GSS) has been used to assess social isolation. Other tools such as the Social Capital Assessment mapping tool and the Social Capital Benchmark Survey have been developed to measure support at the household, community, and organizational levels.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EReferences\u003C\/strong\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E[1] Uchino BN. \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.psych.utah.edu\/people\/people\/uchino\/Publications\/2009%20Uchino%20PPS.pdf\u0022\u003EUnderstanding the Links Between Social Support and Physical Health A Life-Span Perspective With Emphasis on the Separability of Perceived and Received Support\u003C\/a\u003E. these include:\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cul\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003Eindicating severity of poverty, such as \u0026ldquo;near poverty\u0026rdquo; or \u0026ldquo;extreme poverty,\u0026rdquo;\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003Edifferentiating the population under measurement (total population versus children in poverty)\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003Eusing relative versus absolute measures of poverty (state-reference poverty levels versus the federal poverty standard)[1],\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003Emeasuring areas of concentrated poverty (census tracts with poverty rates of 20 percent or more) [2],\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003Eusing the Official Poverty Measure versus the Supplemental Poverty Measure (SPM) which includes in-kind benefits, for example food stamps, as income and subtracts expenses on basic needs such as food, clothing, shelter and utilities.[3]\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\u003C\/ul\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EState-referenced poverty was not associated with mortality or infant mortality, but poverty defined by the federal standard did correlate to these mortality measures.[1] One disadvantage of a relative poverty measure is that it does not indicate an increase in poverty levels when overall incomes decrease, as during a recession.[4] In considering degrees of poverty, researchers have found that extreme poverty has particularly deleterious effects on children\u0026rsquo;s prospects for a healthy life and is less sensitive to economic changes than poverty or near-poverty.[5] Many reports have concluded that a concentration of poverty in neighborhoods can result in higher crime rates, lower quality public schools, poor housing and health conditions, as well as limited access to private services and job opportunities in these areas. [6]\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EThere are a variety of ways to measure income inequality including:\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cul\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003EGini coefficient - This is the most commonly used measure of inequality. The coefficient varies between 0, which reflects complete equality and 1, which indicates complete inequality (one person has all the income or consumption, all others have none).\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003EDecile dispersion ratios, including:\r\n\t\t\u003Cul\u003E\r\n\t\t\t\u003Cli\u003ERatio of the top 20th percentile cut-off for household income\u0026nbsp;to bottom 20th percentile cut-off\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\t\t\u003Cli\u003ERatio of the mean household income of the top 20% to the mean household income of the bottom 20%\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\t\t\u003Cli\u003ERatio of the mean household income of the top 40% to the mean household income of the bottom 40%\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\t\u003C\/ul\u003E\r\n\t\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\u003C\/ul\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EAlthough there are now increasing references to the Gini coefficient in the popular press, the decile dispersion ratios are probably easier to understand than the Gini coefficient. It is fairly easy to understand a statement that says the average income for the top 20% is 5 times the average income of the bottom 20%. The Gini coefficient is more difficult to interpret.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EReferences\u003C\/strong\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E[1] Hillemeier MM, Lynch J, Harper S, Raghunathan T, Kaplan GA. The most commonly measured air pollutants are particulate matter (PM), ozone (O\u003Csub\u003E3\u003C\/sub\u003E), carbon monoxide (CO), sulfur dioxide (SO\u003Csub\u003E2\u003C\/sub\u003E), nitrogen dioxide (NO\u003Csub\u003E2\u003C\/sub\u003E), volatile organic compounds (VOC), lead (Pb), and polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs).[1] They are frequently measured using monitors, but monitoring is often sporadic, making it difficult to estimate the true level of exposure.[2-4]\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EMonitors only measure the air quality at a specific location. They capture point-source pollution well but may not capture the air quality a few miles away or accurately measure nonpoint-source pollution. Point-sources include stationary buildings such as industrial factories. Nonpoint-sources (e.g. motor-vehicle exhaust) have the potential to change location, and may differ based on time of day. For example, air pollutant levels may be higher during rush hour, which is difficult to capture using passive or active monitoring.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EIn order to estimate county or state-level air pollution, modeling techniques or proxy clinical data are utilized. Models such as the Community Multi-Scale Air Quality Model (CMAQ) extrapolate data from monitors, weather patterns, and vehicle emissions to provide large-scale geographic estimates. Usually, threshold levels are used to measure air pollution. Common measures are the number of days the Air Quality Index exceeds 100 or the number of days PM\u003Csub\u003E2.5\u003C\/sub\u003E is measured to be higher than 12.0 micrograms per cubic meter (\u0026micro;g\/m\u003Csup\u003E3\u003C\/sup\u003E). The Air Quality Index is updated daily by the EPA, and provides information at the zip code level.[5] Other proxy air pollution measures include the number of emergency room visits for respiratory diseases such as asthma or the number of diseases associated with air pollution.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EWater quality measures that are generated from actual water quality samples are very reliable. However, collecting water quality samples for all possible contaminants in a large geographic region is not feasible for a statewide or national study. National measures have included measuring the number or percentage of people on public water supplies. Others have used databases to determine specific violation counts for each water system. This has led to a national reporting system to the EPA. The EPA requires states to report when water systems reported Maximum Contaminant Levels higher than the acceptable limit. They maintain a database called SDWIS (Safe Drinking Water Information System), going back to 1993, which houses these violations. The database includes the specific contaminant, date of contamination, length of contamination, and other valuable pieces of information.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EReferences\u003C\/strong\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E[1] Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/ephtracking.cdc.gov\/QueryPanel\/EPHTNQuery\/EPHTQuery.html?c=AQ\u0026amp;i=-1\u0026amp;m=-1\u0022\u003ENational Environmental Public Health Tracking Network\u003C\/a\u003E. Broadly, housing measures cover the affordability, availability, and construction of units. Measures of affordability include the percentage of income devoted to rental\/mortgage costs, total housing costs (rent, heat, electricity), and assessments of Fair Market Value. Availability of housing, whether or not enough housing units exist across all income levels and family units, is often measured using vacancy, overcrowding and homelessness rates. Finally, construction and structural measures include whether hazards such as lead paint, lack of plumbing or kitchen facilities, mold, or indoor air pollution exist in the unit.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EHigh quality transportation systems are safe and low-cost, connect people and freight with the destinations (including jobs and health care) they need to reach, and limit damage to the environment. A\u0026nbsp;2011 report by the Pew Center on the States and The Rockefeller Foundation [1] outlines a number of core performance measures for transportation systems including: traffic crashes, freight travel times\/speeds, traffic congestion\/density, access to jobs and labor, emissions and road conditions.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EAn example of a cross-disciplinary housing and transit measure is the Housing + Transit affordability index which measures differences in location-efficient and inefficient neighborhoods. Efficient neighborhoods have compact, mixed use housing with convenient access to jobs, services, transit, and amenities. These types of neighborhoods tend to have lower transportation costs. The Housing + Transit affordability index also includes a traditional housing affordability measure of housing costs more than 30 percent of the household income. Under this view, three out of four (76 percent) US neighborhoods are considered \u0026ldquo;affordable\u0026rdquo; to the typical household. This index combines both the transportation and housing costs into one index.[2]\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Ch5\u003EReferences\u003C\/h5\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E[1] The Pew Charitable Trusts and The Rockefeller Foundation. \u003Cem\u003E\u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.pewstates.org\/uploadedFiles\/PCS_Assets\/2011\/Measuring_Transportation_Investments.pdf\u0022\u003EMeasuring Transportation Investments: The Road to Results\u003C\/a\u003E\u003C\/em\u003E. relationships and networks, and quality health care. Unfortunately, there are location-based disparities throughout the nation and many people live in communities where leading a healthy lifestyle is difficult.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EThe County Health Rankings, released annually by the University of Wisconsin Population Health Institute and the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation, illustrates these disparities, provides an annual check-up of the health of counties within each state and shows that where we live, learn, work and play matters to our health. The 2012 County Health Rankings will be released this Spring.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cul\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003ERead the full article by Angela Russell via \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.cnu.org\/cnu-salons\/2012\/01\/county-health-rankings\u0022\u003ECongress for the New Urbanism\u003C\/a\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\u003C\/ul\u003E\r\n","format":"full_html","processed":"\u003Cp\u003ELeading a healthy lifestyle is easier if you live in a community that has access to healthy foods, safe and accessible bike paths and sidewalks, a good education system, a strong economy, supportive relationships and networks, and quality health care. Unfortunately, there are location-based disparities throughout the nation and many people live in communities where leading a healthy lifestyle is difficult.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EThe County Health Rankings, released annually by the University of Wisconsin Population Health Institute and the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation, illustrates these disparities, provides an annual check-up of the health of counties within each state and shows that where we live, learn, work and play matters to our health. The 2012 County Health Rankings will be released this Spring.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\t\u003Cli\u003ERead the full article by Angela Russell via \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.cnu.org\/cnu-salons\/2012\/01\/county-health-rankings\u0022\u003ECongress for the New Urbanism\u003C\/a\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\n\u003C\/ul\u003E\n"}},"relationships":{"paragraph_type":{"data":{"type":"paragraphs_type--paragraphs_type","id":"e734b1ee-ccc4-4aee-bf85-23e2b1ea8dee","meta":{"drupal_internal__target_id":"content_block"}},"links":{"related":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/d39d48f8-865b-4bc5-9e00-624584c0d619\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A700"},"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/d39d48f8-865b-4bc5-9e00-624584c0d619\/relationships\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A700"}}}}},{"type":"paragraph--content_block","id":"c280c38d-c328-4693-9dc1-9402703e1b93","links":{"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/c280c38d-c328-4693-9dc1-9402703e1b93?resourceVersion=id%3A701"}},"attributes":{"drupal_internal__id":701,"drupal_internal__revision_id":701,"langcode":"en","status":true,"created":"2019-12-27T18:35:26+00:00","parent_id":"193","parent_type":"node","parent_field_name":"field_page_content","behavior_settings":[],"default_langcode":true,"revision_translation_affected":true,"field_wysiwyg":{"value":"\u003Cp\u003EAs mayor of Kansas City, Kan., Joe Reardon is justifiably proud of the University of Kansas Medical Center, which has trained several generations of physicians and nurses for more than 100 years. After all, the medical center is consistently rated as the best hospital and treatment center in the state, according to a popular ranking of health institutions. So when Mayor Reardon\u0026mdash;who heads the government of both the city and Wyandotte County, in which it sits\u0026mdash;first learned that Wyandotte had come in dead last among the state\u0026rsquo;s counties in a rigorous analysis of health measurements in 2009, he was shocked. \u0026ldquo;We have great access to excellent health care in a state where some counties have essentially no access,\u0026rdquo; Mayor Reardon says. \u0026ldquo;And we\u0026rsquo;re ranked last out of 105 counties? My first reaction was, \u0026lsquo;How could this be?\u0026rsquo;\u0026rdquo;\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EThe answer, Mayor Reardon discovered as he delved into the statistics behind the claim, is that proximity to fine hospitals and first-rate doctors is only one of many factors\u0026mdash;and not always the most important\u0026mdash;determining how long people live and how vulnerable they are to serious illness. Evidence collected by public health experts over the past few decades repeatedly shows that less obvious forces, including proper diet and exercise, higher levels of education, good jobs, greater neighborhood safety, and underlying support from family and friends, provide a powerful, and often unappreciated, boost to a community\u0026rsquo;s health and well-being. By the same token, studies demonstrate, a poor showing in any of these areas can sink the health of individuals or of communities\u0026mdash;even if they have access to topflight medical facilities.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EThe goal of the \u003Cem\u003ECounty Health Rankings\u003C\/em\u003E project, which has given Wyandotte County low marks for health but high praise for its commitment to change, is to bring these hidden health factors to light and thereby help elected officials, civic leaders and community groups take concrete steps that can improve the health of local residents. The initiative originated at the University of Wisconsin\u0026ndash;Madison, covering solely that state in 2003. A similar project began in Kansas in 2009, and in 2010 the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation in Princeton, N.J., provided funding so that the University of Wisconsin could expand its investigation to include within-state comparisons of counties in all 50 states.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cul\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.scientificamerican.com\/article.cfm?id=county-health-rankings\u0022\u003ERead the full Scientific American article\u003C\/a\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\u003C\/ul\u003E\r\n","format":"full_html","processed":"\u003Cp\u003EAs mayor of Kansas City, Kan., Joe Reardon is justifiably proud of the University of Kansas Medical Center, which has trained several generations of physicians and nurses for more than 100 years. After all, the medical center is consistently rated as the best hospital and treatment center in the state, according to a popular ranking of health institutions. So when Mayor Reardon\u2014who heads the government of both the city and Wyandotte County, in which it sits\u2014first learned that Wyandotte had come in dead last among the state\u2019s counties in a rigorous analysis of health measurements in 2009, he was shocked. \u201cWe have great access to excellent health care in a state where some counties have essentially no access,\u201d Mayor Reardon says. \u201cAnd we\u2019re ranked last out of 105 counties? My first reaction was, \u2018How could this be?\u2019\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EThe answer, Mayor Reardon discovered as he delved into the statistics behind the claim, is that proximity to fine hospitals and first-rate doctors is only one of many factors\u2014and not always the most important\u2014determining how long people live and how vulnerable they are to serious illness. Evidence collected by public health experts over the past few decades repeatedly shows that less obvious forces, including proper diet and exercise, higher levels of education, good jobs, greater neighborhood safety, and underlying support from family and friends, provide a powerful, and often unappreciated, boost to a community\u2019s health and well-being. By the same token, studies demonstrate, a poor showing in any of these areas can sink the health of individuals or of communities\u2014even if they have access to topflight medical facilities.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EThe goal of the \u003Cem\u003ECounty Health Rankings\u003C\/em\u003E project, which has given Wyandotte County low marks for health but high praise for its commitment to change, is to bring these hidden health factors to light and thereby help elected officials, civic leaders and community groups take concrete steps that can improve the health of local residents. The initiative originated at the University of Wisconsin\u2013Madison, covering solely that state in 2003. A similar project began in Kansas in 2009, and in 2010 the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation in Princeton, N.J., provided funding so that the University of Wisconsin could expand its investigation to include within-state comparisons of counties in all 50 states.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\t\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.scientificamerican.com\/article.cfm?id=county-health-rankings\u0022\u003ERead the full Scientific American article\u003C\/a\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\n\u003C\/ul\u003E\n"}},"relationships":{"paragraph_type":{"data":{"type":"paragraphs_type--paragraphs_type","id":"e734b1ee-ccc4-4aee-bf85-23e2b1ea8dee","meta":{"drupal_internal__target_id":"content_block"}},"links":{"related":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/c280c38d-c328-4693-9dc1-9402703e1b93\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A701"},"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/c280c38d-c328-4693-9dc1-9402703e1b93\/relationships\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A701"}}}}},{"type":"paragraph--content_block","id":"06c6d53b-496e-4c5e-a46a-b03a6ad90731","links":{"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/06c6d53b-496e-4c5e-a46a-b03a6ad90731?resourceVersion=id%3A702"}},"attributes":{"drupal_internal__id":702,"drupal_internal__revision_id":702,"langcode":"en","status":true,"created":"2019-12-27T18:35:26+00:00","parent_id":"194","parent_type":"node","parent_field_name":"field_page_content","behavior_settings":[],"default_langcode":true,"revision_translation_affected":true,"field_wysiwyg":{"value":"\u003Cp\u003EMany people think of the city lifestyle as unhealthy, associating it with noise, pollution, crime, dense populations, a fast pace, and high stress levels. Many aspire to leaving the city for the country and the healthier lifestyle they think more tranquility brings. Fresh air, open spaces and chirping birds should be conducive to a much healthier lifestyle, or so the thinking goes.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EBut, reports CBS News correspondent Elaine Quijano, a new study seems to dispel those notions. Style Definitions *\/\r\n table.MsoNormalTable\r\n\t{mso-style-name:\u0022Table 5.4pt 0in Roman\u0022;\r\n\tmso-bidi-theme-font:minor-bidi;}\r\n\u003C\/style\u003E\r\n\u003C![endif]--\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EWhen you think about improving the health of people in your community, what comes to mind? Improving access to and the quality of medical services? Yes. Increasing access to recreation opportunities or nutritious food? For sure. Cleaning the air or water? Absolutely. These all are critical ways to improve the health of community members.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EBut, do you think about supporting more students to attend college? Creating jobs? Helping families to achieve more financial stability? Improving your community\u0026rsquo;s safety? These factors may not come as readily to mind, yet they all have a significant impact on health.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EThat\u0026rsquo;s why we are enthused about our new role in the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation \u003Cem\u003E\u003Cspan\u003ECounty Health Rankings \u0026amp; Roadmaps Program\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/em\u003E, an initiative to improve our nation\u0026rsquo;s health by thinking beyond health care, beyond the doctor\u0026rsquo;s office. County Health Rankings, which are issued annually by RWJF and the University of Wisconsin Population Health Institute, pinpoint \u003Cem\u003E\u003Cspan\u003Ewhat we know\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/em\u003E about what makes residents sick or healthy. The Roadmaps show\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E how\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/em\u003E community partnerships are working to create healthier places to live, learn, work and play.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cul\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003ERead the full blog post from \u003Ca \u003C\/w:LatentStyles\u003E\n\u003C\/xml\u003E\u003C![endif]--\u003E\u003C!--[if gte mso 10]\u003E\n\u003Cstyle\u003E\n \/* Style Definitions *\/\n table.MsoNormalTable\n\t{mso-style-name:\u0022Table Normal\u0022;\n\tmso-tstyle-rowband-size:0;\n\tmso-tstyle-colband-size:0;\n\tmso-style-noshow:yes;\n\tmso-style-priority:99;\n\tmso-style-qformat:yes;\n\tmso-style-parent:\u0022\u0022;\n\tmso-padding-alt:0in 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt;\n\tmso-para-margin:0in;\n\tmso-para-margin-bottom:.0001pt;\n\tmso-pagination:widow-orphan;\n\tfont-size:11.0pt;\n\tfont-family:\u0022Calibri\u0022,\u0022sans-serif\u0022;\n\tmso-ascii-font-family:Calibri;\n\tmso-ascii-theme-font:minor-latin;\n\tmso-fareast-font-family:\u0022Times New Roman\u0022;\n\tmso-fareast-theme-font:minor-fareast;\n\tmso-hansi-font-family:Calibri;\n\tmso-hansi-theme-font:minor-latin;\n\tmso-bidi-font-family:\u0022Times New Roman\u0022;\n\tmso-bidi-theme-font:minor-bidi;}\n\u003C\/style\u003E\n\u003C![endif]--\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EWhen you think about improving the health of people in your community, what comes to mind? Improving access to and the quality of medical services? Yes. Increasing access to recreation opportunities or nutritious food? For sure. Cleaning the air or water? Absolutely. These all are critical ways to improve the health of community members.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EBut, do you think about supporting more students to attend college? Creating jobs? Helping families to achieve more financial stability? Improving your community\u2019s safety? These factors may not come as readily to mind, yet they all have a significant impact on health.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EThat\u2019s why we are enthused about our new role in the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation \u003Cem\u003E\u003Cspan\u003ECounty Health Rankings \u0026amp; Roadmaps Program\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/em\u003E, an initiative to improve our nation\u2019s health by thinking beyond health care, beyond the doctor\u2019s office. County Health Rankings, which are issued annually by RWJF and the University of Wisconsin Population Health Institute, pinpoint \u003Cem\u003E\u003Cspan\u003Ewhat we know\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/em\u003E about what makes residents sick or healthy. The Roadmaps show\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E how\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/em\u003E community partnerships are working to create healthier places to live, learn, work and play.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\t\u003Cli\u003ERead the full blog post from \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/blog.communitycatalyst.org\/index.php\/2011\/11\/14\/communities-create-a-roadmap-to-health\/\u0022\u003ECommunity Catalyst\u003C\/a\u003E\u003C\/li\u003E\n\u003C\/ul\u003E\n"}},"relationships":{"paragraph_type":{"data":{"type":"paragraphs_type--paragraphs_type","id":"e734b1ee-ccc4-4aee-bf85-23e2b1ea8dee","meta":{"drupal_internal__target_id":"content_block"}},"links":{"related":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/60de60d0-03c0-4984-bae8-8a58a637bd67\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A703"},"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/60de60d0-03c0-4984-bae8-8a58a637bd67\/relationships\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A703"}}}}},{"type":"paragraph--content_block","id":"d5b20e46-d77c-46c6-8dc4-a63101b60263","links":{"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/d5b20e46-d77c-46c6-8dc4-a63101b60263?resourceVersion=id%3A704"}},"attributes":{"drupal_internal__id":704,"drupal_internal__revision_id":704,"langcode":"en","status":true,"created":"2019-12-27T18:35:26+00:00","parent_id":"196","parent_type":"node","parent_field_name":"field_page_content","behavior_settings":[],"default_langcode":true,"revision_translation_affected":true,"field_wysiwyg":{"value":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EGrantees Working with Community Partners to Address Impact of Employment, Education and Other Factors that Affect Health\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/em\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMadison, WI and Princeton, NJ\u003C\/strong\u003E (November 9, 2011)\u2014Twelve coalitions across the United States have been awarded Roadmaps to Health Community Grants of up to $200,000 by the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation (RWJF) to improve the health of people living in those communities. The grants, which will support two-year state and local efforts among policymakers, business, education, health care, public health and community organizations, will be managed by Community Catalyst and are part of the County Health Rankings \u0026amp; Roadmaps program, a collaboration of RWJF and the University of Wisconsin Population Health Institute (UWPHI).\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThese projects are really exciting for two reasons: \u0026nbsp;first, they are truly collaborations among diverse community partners who bring expertise, relationships and resources to improve people\u2019s health; and second, that in addition to \u0026nbsp;health behaviors such as smoking and diet, they are focused on factors such as education, family relationships and income that heavily influence our health and quality of life,\u201d said James S. Marks, MD, MPH, senior vice president and director of the Health Group at RWJF.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EHaving health insurance and quality health care \u0026nbsp;are \u0026nbsp;important \u2013 but we know that much of what influences our health happens outside of the doctor\u2019s office. The grantees will build on the RWJF\/UWPHI County Health Rankings model, which highlights the critical role that factors such as education, jobs and our environment play in influencing how healthy people are and how long they live. The Roadmaps to Health grantees are working to create healthier places to live, learn, work and play. Taken together, the Rankings and action of the Roadmaps to Health grantees are a way of solving our nation\u2019s health crisis.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EIn addition to the Community Grants, the County Health Rankings \u0026amp; Roadmaps program will include other efforts to mobilize local communities and national partners and leaders, including grants to national organizations to activate local leaders and affiliates to improve health; a prize program to recognize communities taking action whose promising efforts will lead to better health; and, tools and resources to help groups working to improve the health of their communities.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EThe 12 Roadmaps to Health projects selected as grantees were chosen from more than 300 initial applicants. The grantees will build consensus for and implement policy and systems changes that address the factors that we know are connected to health \u2013 education, income and employment, community safety and social connectedness. In Alameda County (Calif.), efforts will make more consumer-focused banking services available to residents of low-income neighborhoods and will also educate residents about the health and economic benefits of these services, such as using bank accounts without minimum balance requirements, instead of check-cashing or bill-payment services with fees. A project in Kentucky will build state-wide support for tax policies that will help low-income families in that state retain more of their income\u2014money that can then be spent on health improvements, like regular doctor\u2019s visits, healthier diets, and weight-loss or smoking cessation services.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EIn New Mexico, the focus will be on efforts to prepare all children in the state equally for academic, health, and economic success by advocating for policies that create, fund and sustain a high-quality, universally accessible continuum of early childhood care, health, and education services. Community Catalyst, a national consumer health advocacy organization, will use its expertise and successful advocacy framework of coalition building, grassroots mobilization, policy research analysis, campaign design and implementation, and communications to help each grantee group achieve results, including policy and systemic changes.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EDetailed descriptions of all 12 grant recipients are available \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.countyhealthrankings.org\/about-project\/community-grants\u0022\u003Ehere\u003C\/a\u003E. \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E###\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAbout the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation\u003C\/strong\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EThe Robert Wood Johnson Foundation focuses on the pressing health and health care issues facing our country. As the nation\u0027s largest philanthropy devoted exclusively to health and health care, the Foundation works with a diverse group of organizations and individuals to identify solutions and achieve comprehensive, measureable and timely change. For nearly 40 years the Foundation has brought experience, commitment, and a rigorous, balanced approach to the problems that affect the health and health care of those it serves. When it comes to helping Americans lead healthier lives and get the care they need, the Foundation expects to make a difference in your lifetime. The Institute strives to:\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cul\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003EAddress a broad range of real world problems of topical importance to government, business, providers, and the public;\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003EPromote partnerships of inquiry between researchers and users of research, breaking down barriers between the academic community and public and private sector policy makers;\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003EAdvance the development of interdisciplinary research, along the spectrum from public health to health care;\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003EProvide continuing education for practitioners and opportunities for applied learning for graduate and medical students; and\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\t\u003Cli\u003EMake useful contributions to public health and health policy decisions that improve the health of the public.\u003C\/li\u003E\r\n\u003C\/ul\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EFor more information, visit \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/uwphi.pophealth.wisc.edu\u0022\u003Ehttp:\/\/uwphi.pophealth.wisc.edu\u003C\/a\u003E.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAbout Community Catalyst\u003C\/strong\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003ECommunity Catalyst is a national non-profit consumer advocacy organization dedicated to quality affordable health care for all. Follow us on Twitter @\u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/twitter.com\/#!\/HealthPolicyHub\u0022\u003Ehealthpolicyhub\u003C\/a\u003E.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n","format":"full_html","processed":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EGrantees Working with Community Partners to Address Impact of Employment, Education and Other Factors that Affect Health\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/em\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMadison, WI and Princeton, NJ\u003C\/strong\u003E (November 9, 2011)\u2014Twelve coalitions across the United States have been awarded Roadmaps to Health Community Grants of up to $200,000 by the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation (RWJF) to improve the health of people living in those communities. The grants, which will support two-year state and local efforts among policymakers, business, education, health care, public health and community organizations, will be managed by Community Catalyst and are part of the County Health Rankings \u0026amp; Roadmaps program, a collaboration of RWJF and the University of Wisconsin Population Health Institute (UWPHI).\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThese projects are really exciting for two reasons: \u0026nbsp;first, they are truly collaborations among diverse community partners who bring expertise, relationships and resources to improve people\u2019s health; and second, that in addition to \u0026nbsp;health behaviors such as smoking and diet, they are focused on factors such as education, family relationships and income that heavily influence our health and quality of life,\u201d said James S. Marks, MD, MPH, senior vice president and director of the Health Group at RWJF.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EHaving health insurance and quality health care \u0026nbsp;are \u0026nbsp;important \u2013 but we know that much of what influences our health happens outside of the doctor\u2019s office. The grantees will build on the RWJF\/UWPHI County Health Rankings model, which highlights the critical role that factors such as education, jobs and our environment play in influencing how healthy people are and how long they live. The Roadmaps to Health grantees are working to create healthier places to live, learn, work and play. Taken together, the Rankings and action of the Roadmaps to Health grantees are a way of solving our nation\u2019s health crisis.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EIn addition to the Community Grants, the County Health Rankings \u0026amp; Roadmaps program will include other efforts to mobilize local communities and national partners and leaders, including grants to national organizations to activate local leaders and affiliates to improve health; a prize program to recognize communities taking action whose promising efforts will lead to better health; and, tools and resources to help groups working to improve the health of their communities.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EThe 12 Roadmaps to Health projects selected as grantees were chosen from more than 300 initial applicants. The grantees will build consensus for and implement policy and systems changes that address the factors that we know are connected to health \u2013 education, income and employment, community safety and social connectedness. In Alameda County (Calif.), efforts will make more consumer-focused banking services available to residents of low-income neighborhoods and will also educate residents about the health and economic benefits of these services, such as using bank accounts without minimum balance requirements, instead of check-cashing or bill-payment services with fees. A project in Kentucky will build state-wide support for tax policies that will help low-income families in that state retain more of their income\u2014money that can then be spent on health improvements, like regular doctor\u2019s visits, healthier diets, and weight-loss or smoking cessation services.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EIn New Mexico, the focus will be on efforts to prepare all children in the state equally for academic, health, and economic success by advocating for policies that create, fund and sustain a high-quality, universally accessible continuum of early childhood care, health, and education services. Community Catalyst, a national consumer health advocacy organization, will use its expertise and successful advocacy framework of coalition building, grassroots mobilization, policy research analysis, campaign design and implementation, and communications to help each grantee group achieve results, including policy and systemic changes.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EDetailed descriptions of all 12 grant recipients are available \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.countyhealthrankings.org\/about-project\/community-grants\u0022\u003Ehere\u003C\/a\u003E. \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003E###\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAbout the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation\u003C\/strong\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EThe Robert Wood Johnson Foundation focuses on the pressing health and health care issues facing our country. As the nation\u0027s largest philanthropy devoted exclusively to health and health care, the Foundation works with a diverse group of organizations and individuals to identify solutions and achieve comprehensive, measureable and timely change. For nearly 40 years the Foundation has brought experience, commitment, and a rigorous, balanced approach to the problems that affect the health and health care of those it serves. When it comes to helping Americans lead healthier lives and get the care they need, the Foundation expects to make a difference in your lifetime. Style Definitions *\/\r\n table.MsoNormalTable\r\n\t{mso-style-name:\u0022Table 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt;\r\n\tmso-para-margin:0in;\r\n\tmso-para-margin-bottom:.0001pt;\r\n\tmso-pagination:widow-orphan;\r\n\tfont-size:11.0pt;\r\n\tfont-family:\u0022Calibri\u0022,\u0022sans-serif\u0022;\r\n\tmso-ascii-font-family:Calibri;\r\n\tmso-ascii-theme-font:minor-latin;\r\n\tmso-fareast-font-family:\u0022Times Roman\u0022;\r\n\tmso-bidi-theme-font:minor-bidi;}\r\n\u003C\/style\u003E\r\n\u003C![endif]--\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003ETis the season. Tomatoes are ripening, peaches are juicy, and the zucchini just won\u0026#39;t quit. There\u0026#39;s nothing like fruit and veggies fresh from the garden, for the taste buds and for your health. U.S. Department of Agriculture Secretary Tom Vilsack knows the importance of fresh foods in our diets and named this week National Farmers Market Week. Markets across the country are hosting events to raise awareness of the crucial need farmers markets meet.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EStudies consistently show that diets rich in fruits and vegetables can help prevent heart disease, stroke, obesity, some types of cancer and control blood pressure. The\u0026nbsp;\u003Ci\u003ECounty Health Rankings\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/i\u003Emeasure access to healthy food and one of the best ways to access nutritious foods is through farmers markets. Farmers markets provide people with fresh, local options and facilitate\u0026nbsp;strong\u0026nbsp;relationships between farmers and communities.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EAs the\u0026nbsp;\u003Ci\u003ERankings\u003C\/i\u003E\u0026nbsp;show us, easily accessible healthful food options are essential for community health. Not surprisingly, people eat what is available to them. And too many counties have\u0026nbsp;few or no options for\u0026nbsp;readily accessible and healthy food.\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003ETo help communities see how many of their residents have access to these foods, the\u0026nbsp;\u003Ci\u003ERankings\u003C\/i\u003E\u0026nbsp;provide a national benchmark of 92%--only 10% of counties have a higher percent of zip codes with healthy food outlets.\u0026nbsp;\u003Cspan style=\u0022-webkit-tap-highlight-color: rgba(26, 26, 26, 0.296875);-webkit-composition-fill-color: rgba(175, 192, 227, 0.230469);\r\n-webkit-composition-frame-color: rgba(77, 128, 180, 0.230469)\u0022\u003E\u003Cspan class=\u0022apple-style-span\u0022\u003EState by state, the numbers are shocking. In some counties, less than 25% of zip codes have healthy food outlets.\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\u0022-webkit-tap-highlight-color: rgba(26, 26, 26, 0.296875);-webkit-composition-fill-color: rgba(175, 192, 227, 0.230469);\r\n-webkit-composition-frame-color: rgba(77, 128, 180, 0.230469)\u0022\u003E\u003Cspan class=\u0022apple-style-span\u0022\u003EWhat\u0026rsquo;s more, a recent\u0026nbsp;\u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/healthland.time.com\/2011\/08\/04\/how-to-make-a-healthy-diet-more-affordable\/\u0022\u003E\u003Ci\u003EHealth Affairs\u003C\/i\u003E\u0026nbsp;study\u003C\/a\u003E shows that even if fresh, healthy options are available, many people simply cannot afford them. Researchers from the University of Washington found that when people who primarily eat processed food increase consumption of fruits and vegetables, whole grains and low-fat dairy products, their grocery bills go up.\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003EFarmers markets don\u0026rsquo;t only promote good health; they are affordable because consumers are buying seasonal foods directly from the source. Communities should promote and expand access to local options for fresh food like farmers markets. Learn more about the\u003Ca href=\u0022\/our-approach\/health-factors\/built-environment\u0022\u003E limited access to healthy\u0026nbsp;food measure\u003C\/a\u003E, and then go find yourself a farmers market and grab some of those tomatoes while they\u0026#39;re still in season.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\r\n\r\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EImage 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt;\n\tmso-para-margin:0in;\n\tmso-para-margin-bottom:.0001pt;\n\tmso-pagination:widow-orphan;\n\tfont-size:11.0pt;\n\tfont-family:\u0022Calibri\u0022,\u0022sans-serif\u0022;\n\tmso-ascii-font-family:Calibri;\n\tmso-ascii-theme-font:minor-latin;\n\tmso-fareast-font-family:\u0022Times Roman\u0022;\n\tmso-bidi-theme-font:minor-bidi;}\n\u003C\/style\u003E\n\u003C![endif]--\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003ETis the season. Tomatoes are ripening, peaches are juicy, and the zucchini just won\u0027t quit. There\u0027s nothing like fruit and veggies fresh from the garden, for the taste buds and for your health. U.S. Department of Agriculture Secretary Tom Vilsack knows the importance of fresh foods in our diets and named this week National Farmers Market Week. Markets across the country are hosting events to raise awareness of the crucial need farmers markets meet.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EStudies consistently show that diets rich in fruits and vegetables can help prevent heart disease, stroke, obesity, some types of cancer and control blood pressure. The\u0026nbsp;\u003Ci\u003ECounty Health Rankings\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/i\u003Emeasure access to healthy food and one of the best ways to access nutritious foods is through farmers markets. Farmers markets provide people with fresh, local options and facilitate\u0026nbsp;strong\u0026nbsp;relationships between farmers and communities.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EAs the\u0026nbsp;\u003Ci\u003ERankings\u003C\/i\u003E\u0026nbsp;show us, easily accessible healthful food options are essential for community health. Not surprisingly, people eat what is available to them. And too many counties have\u0026nbsp;few or no options for\u0026nbsp;readily accessible and healthy food.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003ETo help communities see how many of their residents have access to these foods, the\u0026nbsp;\u003Ci\u003ERankings\u003C\/i\u003E\u0026nbsp;provide a national benchmark of 92%--only 10% of counties have a higher percent of zip codes with healthy food outlets.\u0026nbsp;\u003Cspan style=\u0022-webkit-tap-highlight-color: rgba(26, 26, 26, 0.296875);-webkit-composition-fill-color: rgba(175, 192, 227, 0.230469);\n-webkit-composition-frame-color: rgba(77, 128, 180, 0.230469)\u0022\u003E\u003Cspan class=\u0022apple-style-span\u0022\u003EState by state, the numbers are shocking. In some counties, less than 25% of zip codes have healthy food outlets.\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan style=\u0022-webkit-tap-highlight-color: rgba(26, 26, 26, 0.296875);-webkit-composition-fill-color: rgba(175, 192, 227, 0.230469);\n-webkit-composition-frame-color: rgba(77, 128, 180, 0.230469)\u0022\u003E\u003Cspan class=\u0022apple-style-span\u0022\u003EWhat\u2019s more, a recent\u0026nbsp;\u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/healthland.time.com\/2011\/08\/04\/how-to-make-a-healthy-diet-more-affordable\/\u0022\u003E\u003Ci\u003EHealth Affairs\u003C\/i\u003E\u0026nbsp;study\u003C\/a\u003E shows that even if fresh, healthy options are available, many people simply cannot afford them. Researchers from the University of Washington found that when people who primarily eat processed food increase consumption of fruits and vegetables, whole grains and low-fat dairy products, their grocery bills go up.\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/span\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003EFarmers markets don\u2019t only promote good health; they are affordable because consumers are buying seasonal foods directly from the source. Communities should promote and expand access to local options for fresh food like farmers markets. Learn more about the\u003Ca href=\u0022\/our-approach\/health-factors\/built-environment\u0022\u003E limited access to healthy\u0026nbsp;food measure\u003C\/a\u003E, and then go find yourself a farmers market and grab some of those tomatoes while they\u0027re still in season.\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003E\u0026nbsp;\u003C\/p\u003E\n\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EImage courtesy of \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/nataliemaynor\/\u0022\u003ENatalieMaynor\u0027s photostream\u003C\/a\u003E, Flickr.com Creative Commons\u003C\/em\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},"relationships":{"paragraph_type":{"data":{"type":"paragraphs_type--paragraphs_type","id":"e734b1ee-ccc4-4aee-bf85-23e2b1ea8dee","meta":{"drupal_internal__target_id":"content_block"}},"links":{"related":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/53578293-e90d-4eda-9fa5-60d1e8d6d62b\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A719"},"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/53578293-e90d-4eda-9fa5-60d1e8d6d62b\/relationships\/paragraph_type?resourceVersion=id%3A719"}}}}},{"type":"paragraph--content_block","id":"591858eb-644c-4398-8e2a-ef38d379eb6c","links":{"self":{"href":"https:\/\/klatovak.github.io\/pages-test\/jsonapi\/paragraph\/content_block\/591858eb-644c-4398-8e2a-ef38d379eb6c?resourceVersion=id%3A720"}},"attributes":{"drupal_internal__id":720,"drupal_internal__revision_id":720,"langcode":"en","status":true,"created":"2019-12-27T18:35:26+00:00","parent_id":"212","parent_type":"node","parent_field_name":"field_page_content","behavior_settings":[],"default_langcode":true,"revision_translation_affected":true,"field_wysiwyg":{"value":"\u003Cp\u003EFor decades Baltimore has been plagued with high rates of serious illnesses such as diabetes, heart disease and HIV\/AIDS, and struggled with social problems including unemployment, poverty and violence that often lead to poor health.
